The Hadith of Ghadir Khumm: Proof and Refutation
Presentation and refutation of Hadith al-Ghadir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia use of Hadith al-Ghadir to argue for Ali's immediate Imamate, and the Sunni refutation that the hadith only indicates worthiness, not exclusive succession.

Core Idea
Hadith al-Ghadir proves Ali's worthiness, not his immediate rule.

The Hadith of Ghadir Khumm is one of the most cited proofs for the Imamate of Ali in Shia theology. However, Sunni scholars argue that the hadith does not establish immediate succession over the first three caliphs. In this lesson, we examine the hadith text, the Shia interpretation, and the Sunni counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic text of the hadith and the accompanying translation. Note the key phrase 'man kuntu mawlahu fa-Aliyyun mawlahu'.
The hadith is widely accepted by both Sunnis and Shias.
2
Understand the Shia argument
The Shia argue that 'mawlā' here means 'the one most entitled to authority' (al-awlā bi-l-tasarruf), making Ali the immediate successor. They dismiss other meanings as impossible or irrelevant.
They use the logic that the Prophet's question 'Am I not awlā to you?' forces the meaning of 'mawlā' to ruler.
3
Examine the Sunni refutation
The refutation points out that even if 'mawlā' means ruler, the hadith only shows Ali's worthiness at that moment, not that he must be the first caliph. It does not negate the Imamate of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman.
Additional points: the word 'mawlā' has many meanings, and the context may simply emphasize love and support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما السنة فمن وجوه الأول : خبر الغدير وهو أنه عليه السلام أحضر القوم ) بعد رجوعه من حجة الوداع بغدير خم وهو موضع بين مكة والمدينة بالجحفة وأمر بجمع الرحال فصعد عليها، وقال لهم : أولست أولى بكم من أنفسكم قالوا بلى قال فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه وانصر من نصره وأخذل من خذله، وجه الاستدلال أن المراد بالمولى ) هاهنا ( هو الأولى ليطابق مقدمة الحديث ولأنه ) أي : لفظ المولى ( يقال للمعتق والمعتق وابن العم والجار والحليف والناصر والأولى بالتصرف والسنة الأولى غير مرادة ) هاهنا ( قطعاً)، فإن الحمل على المعتق والجار وابن العم يؤدي إلى الكذب والنبي عليه السلام لم يكن معتقاً ولا حليفاً لأحد، والحمل على الناصر ممتنع فإن كل واحد يعلم من دينه ضرورة وجوب تولي المؤمنين بعضهم لبعض فتعين الحمل على الأولى بالتصرف لما ذكرناه ( ولأنها ) أي المعاني المذكورة ) تشترك في الولاية فيجب قوله : (الأول) خبر الغدير إلخ فيه أن غايته الدلالة على استحقاقه الإمامة في الحال لكن من أين يلزم نفي إمامة الأئمة الثلاثة قبله كما هو مدعاهم . قوله : (يؤدي إلى الكذب) فإن علياً رضي الله عنه ليس بمعتق لمن كان النبي الله معتقاً له وهو ظاهر ولا جاراً لمن كان النبي عليه السلام جاراً له ولا ابن عم لمن كان النبي عليه السلام ابن عم له فإنه عليه السلام ابن عم لجعفر وعلي ليس كذلك بل ابن أبيه لأنه أخوه . قوله : ( فإن كل أحد يعلم من دينه إلخ) قيل : يجوز أن يكون الغرض التنصيص على موالاته ونصرته ليكون أبعد عن التخصيص الذي يحتمله أكثر العمومات وليكون أولى بإفادة الشرف حيث قرن بموالاة النبي عليه السلام وهو ظاهر.
As for the Sunnah, it is from aspects. The first: The report of Ghadīr, which is that he (peace be upon him) gathered the people after returning from the Farewell Pilgrimage at Ghadīr Khumm, a place between Mecca and Medina near al-Juhfah. He ordered to gather the saddles, ascended them, and said to them: 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' They said: 'Yes.' He said: 'For whoever I am his mawlā, Ali is his mawlā. O God, befriend whoever befriends him and oppose whoever opposes him, and help whoever helps him and forsake whoever forsakes him.' The point of evidence is that what is meant by mawlā here is the one most entitled (al-awlā), to match the introduction of the hadith. And because the word mawlā is said for the freed slave, the emancipator, the cousin, the neighbor, the ally, the helper, and the one most entitled to dispose. And the first (meanings) are certainly not intended here, for interpreting it as freed slave, neighbor, or cousin would lead to falsehood, since the Prophet (peace be upon him) was not an emancipator nor an ally of anyone. And interpreting it as helper is impossible, for everyone knows from his religion the necessity of believers helping one another. Therefore, it is determined to mean the one most entitled to dispose for the reasons mentioned. And because those meanings share in al-wilāyah (authority or allegiance), it is necessary. His statement: 'The first: The report of Ghadīr etc.' There is that its utmost indication is his worthiness for the Imamate in the present, but from where does it necessitate negating the Imamate of the three caliphs before him as they claim? His statement: 'Leads to falsehood' because Ali (may God be pleased with him) is not a freed slave of the Prophet's freed slave, which is obvious, nor a neighbor of the Prophet's neighbor, nor a cousin of the Prophet's cousin. Indeed, the Prophet (peace be upon him) was a cousin of Ja‘far, and Ali is not, rather he is the son of his uncle? Actually he is his brother? In any case, it leads to falsehood. His statement: 'For everyone knows from his religion etc.' It is said: It is possible that the purpose is to specify his allegiance and support to be further from the specification that most general statements can bear, and to be more worthy of conveying honor, as it is linked to the Prophet's allegiance. And it is obvious.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunnah argument, specifically the famous Hadīth of Ghadīr Khumm. The Shia argue that in this hadith, the Prophet declared Ali as mawlā, meaning 'the one most entitled to authority' (الأولى بالتصرف), thus designating him as successor. They reason that other meanings of mawlā (freed slave, cousin, neighbor, ally, helper) are not applicable: the Prophet was not a freed slave or ally of anyone, and the meaning of 'helper' is too general, as all believers are helpers of each other. Therefore, mawlā must mean 'the one in charge'. The refutation (in the commentary) points out that the hadith only indicates Ali's worthiness (استحقاق) for Imamate at that time, but does not prove that he must be the immediate successor, negating the Imamate of the three caliphs before him. The marginal commentary also addresses the claim that interpreting mawlā as other than 'ruler' leads to falsehood, explaining that Ali is not related in those specific relationships to the Prophet. The final note suggests that the purpose of the hadith might be to emphasize his honor and the obligation of support, not necessarily political succession. Thus the hadith does not constitute a clear proof for immediate succession.

Key Terms
المولى
الأولى بالتصرف
خبر الغدير
الإمامة
الاستحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Ghadir Khumm proves that Ali must be the immediate caliph after the Prophet.
Correction: The hadith only indicates Ali's worthiness for Imamate, not that he must precede the first three caliphs. Worthiness does not entail immediate succession.

Checkpoint
What is the main counterargument against the use of Hadīth al-Ghadīr to prove Ali's immediate Imamate?
A) The hadith is not authentic
B) The hadith only shows Ali's worthiness, not that he must be the first caliph
C) The hadith actually supports Abu Bakr
D) The word 'mawlā' only means 'friend'

Key Takeaway
Hadith al-Ghadir shows Ali's merit but does not establish his immediate Imamate over the first three caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a Companion present at Ghadir Khumm and heard the Prophet's words, how would you have understood them? What does your interpretation reveal about your own views on leadership and succession?
?
The refutation argues that the hadith only shows worthiness, not immediate succession. Is there a part of you that wishes the hadith were more explicit to settle the matter? What does that wish say about your trust in God's plan for the caliphate?
?
Consider your own allegiance to the first three caliphs. If you were convinced that Ali was the most worthy immediately after the Prophet, would your conviction remain the same? Examine your heart: is your stance based on evidence or on tradition? How would you defend it before God?

Challenging the Authenticity of the Ghadir Hadith
The author denies the authenticity and tawātur of the Ghadir hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni objections to the authenticity and transmission of the Ghadir hadith as a proof for Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
The Ghadir hadith's authenticity is disputed, not universally accepted.

The Shia rely on the Ghadir hadith as a central proof for Ali's imamate. However, Sunni scholars have raised several objections concerning its authenticity and transmission. In this passage, the author presents these criticisms, noting that major hadith collections omitted it and prominent scholars questioned its reliability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's argument structure: he denies the hadith's authenticity and supports it with specific evidence.
2
Identify the arguments against authenticity
Find three points: (1) the claim of tawātur is invalid, (2) major hadith scholars did not transmit it, (3) some scholars criticized it. Also note the point about Ali's absence.
3
Consider the rebuttal about Ali's absence
The author mentions that Ali was in Yemen, but notes that this does not necessarily invalidate the hadith unless a specific narration form is assumed.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If the hadith is not authentic, what does that do to the Shia argument? How does this connect to the broader sectarian debate?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الإمام بعد رسول الله
الحمل عليها وجعل اللفظ حقيقة في هذا القدر المشترك (دفعاً للاشتراك) اللفظي . ( الجواب : منع صحة الحديث ودعوى الضرورة) في العلم بصحته لكونه متواتراً مكابرة كيف ولم ينقله أكثر أصحاب الحديث) كالبخاري ومسلم وأضرابهما، وقد طعن بعضهم فيه كابن أبي داود السجستاني وأبي حاتم الرازي وغيرهما من أئمة الحديث، (ولأن عليا لم يكن يوم الغدير مع النبي فإنه كان باليمن، ورد هذا بأن غيبته لا تنافي صحة الحديث إلا أن يروى هكذا: أخذ بيد علي أو استحضره وقال : ( وإن سلم ( أن هذا الحديث صحيح ( فرواته ) أي : أكثرهم (لم يرووا مقدمة الحديث وهي ألست أولى بكم من أنفسكم فلا يمكن أن يتمسك بها في أن المولى بمعنى الأولى . ( والمراد بالمولى ) هو ( الناصر بدليل آخر الحديث ) وهو قوله : ( وال من والاه إلخ ) ، ولأن مفعل بمعنى أفعل لم يذكره أحد ) من أئمة العربية وقوله تعالى : ومأواكم النار ) [العنكبوت : ٢٥ ]، هي مولاكم، أي: مقركم وما إليه مآلكم وعاقبتكم ولهذا قال الله تعالى : وبئس المصير [ الحديد : ١٥ ] ، وقد قيل : المراد هاهنا أيضا الناصر فيكون مبالغة في نفي النصرة على طريقة قولهم : الجوع زاد من لا زاد له ( و ) الاستعمال أيضا يدل على أن المولى ليس بمعنى الأولى . ( لجواز) أن يقال : ( هو أولى من كذا دون مولى من كذا و) أن يقال ( أولى الرجلين أو الرجال دون مولى ( الرجلين أو الرجال ( وإن سلم ) أن المولى بمعنى الأولى ( فأين الدليل على أن المراد الأولى بالتصرف والتدبير بل يجوز أن يراد الأولى ( في أمر من الأمور كما قال الله تعالى : إن أولى الناس بإبراهيم للذين اتبعوه ﴾ [آل عمران : ٦٨]، وأراد الأولوية في الاتباع والاختصاص به والقرب منه لا في التصرف فيه ) وتقول التلامذة : نحن أولى بأستاذنا ويقول الأتباع : نحن أولى بسلطاننا ) ولا يريدون الأولوية في التصرف والتدبير بل في أمر ما والصحة ( الاستفسار ) إذ يجوز أن يقال في أي شيء هو أولى في نصرته أو محبته أو التصرف فيه (و) لصحة ( التقسيم) بأن يقال : كون فلان أولى بزيد إما في نصرته وإما في ضبط أمواله وإما في تدبيره والتصرف فيه وحينئذ لا يدل الحديث على إمامته.
قوله : ( ولأن مفعل بمعنى أفعل لم يذكره أحد أجيب عنه بأن المولى بمعنى المتولي والمالك للأمر، والأولى بالتصرف شائع في كلام العرب منقول من أئمة اللغة، قال أبو عبيدة هي موليك أي أولى بكم، وقال عليه السلام أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن مولاها أي الأولى بها، والمالك لتدبير أمرها ثم المراد أنه اسم لهذا المعنى لا صفة بمنزلة الأولى ليعترض بأنه ليس من صيفة اسم التفضيل وأنه لا يستعمل استعماله .
The Fourth Observatory - The Fourth Objective: The Imam after the Messenger of Allah. [It is argued] to apply [the word] to this common meaning and to make the word literal in this common denominator (to avoid verbal homonymy). (The answer: denial of the authenticity of the hadith and the claim of necessity) of knowledge of its authenticity due to its being mutawātir is a stubborn [claim]—how [can it be] when most hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim and their peers did not transmit it? Indeed, some of them criticized it, such as Ibn Abī Dāwūd al-Sijistānī and Abū Ḥātim al-Rāzī and other imams of hadith. (And because ʿAlī was not with the Prophet on the day of Ghadīr, for he was in Yemen. This was refuted by [saying] that his absence does not negate the authenticity of the hadith unless it is narrated thus: 'He took ʿAlī's hand' or 'summoned him and said...'). (Even if it is granted) that this hadith is authentic, (its narrators)—i.e., most of them—(did not narrate the preamble of the hadith, namely 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' So it is not possible to rely on it for [the claim] that mawlā means awlā. (What is meant by al-mawlā) is (al-nāṣir [the helper], as evidenced by the rest of the hadith), namely his saying: 'And the mawlā of whoever takes him as mawlā,' etc. And because the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal has not been mentioned by anyone among the authorities on Arabic. And His exalted saying: 'and your abode is the Fire' [al-ʿAnkabūt 29:25] 'are your mawlā', meaning your abode and your ultimate destination and end, which is why God said: 'and the evil destination' [al-Ḥadīd 57:15]. And it has been said: what is meant here is also al-nāṣir, so it would be an exaggeration in negating support, in the manner of their saying: 'Hunger is the provision of one who has no provision.' (And) usage also indicates that mawlā is not in the sense of awlā. (Because it is permissible) to say: 'He is awlā (more entitled) than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so', and it is permissible to say 'the awlā of the two men' or 'of the men' but not 'the mawlā of the two men' or 'of the men'. (Even if it is granted) that mawlā means awlā, (where is the proof that what is meant is the awlā in disposal and administration? Rather, it is possible that what is meant is awlā in some matter, as God said: 'Indeed, the people most entitled (awlā) to Abraham are those who followed him' [Āl ʿImrān 3:68], and He meant priority in following, special attachment to him, and closeness to him, not disposal over him.) And the students say: 'We are awlā to our teacher,' and the followers say: 'We are awlā to our sultan,' and they do not mean priority in disposal and administration, but in some matter. And [what supports it is] the validity of (istifsār [asking for clarification]) because it is possible to say: 'In what thing is he awlā? In his support, his love, or disposal over him?' (And) the validity of (taqsīm [division]) because it is possible to say: 'So-and-so's being awlā to Zayd is either in his support, or in controlling his wealth, or in his management and disposal over him.' And then the hadith does not indicate his imāma. His saying: 'And because no one mentioned the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal'—it was answered that al-mawlā means al-mutawallī (the one who takes charge) and al-mālik (the owner) of the affair, and al-awlā bi-l-taṣarruf (more entitled to disposal) is common in Arab speech, transmitted from language scholars. Abu ʿUbayda said: 'hiya mawlīkum' meaning 'awla bikum' (more entitled to you), and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her mawlā' meaning the one more entitled to her (awlā bihā), the owner of the management of her affair. Then what is meant is that it is a noun for this meaning, not a descriptive term like awlā, so that it might be objected that it is not from the form of the elative (ism al-tafḍīl) and that it is not used as they are used.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the Shia argument that the hadith of Ghadīr (man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu) proves that ʿAlī is the rightful imam after the Prophet. The author presents a series of objections and answers. First, he denies the hadith's authenticity, pointing out that many major hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim did not transmit it, and some even criticized it. He also notes that ʿAlī was not present at Ghadīr Khumm because he was in Yemen, though this is countered by saying absence does not preclude the hadith being narrated otherwise. Second, even if the hadith is authentic, most narrators omit the preamble 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' which is crucial for interpreting mawlā as 'more entitled' (awla). Third, the word mawlā in the hadith means 'helper' (nāṣir), as indicated by the continuation of the hadith. Fourth, linguistically, the pattern mufʿal does not mean afʿal, and usage shows that mawlā is not synonymous with awlā: one can say 'He is awlā than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so'. Fifth, even if mawlā is taken to mean awlā, it could refer to priority in some specific matter like support or love, not necessarily in authority and disposal. The author illustrates this with examples from the Quran and common speech. Finally, a linguistic objection about mufʿal being in the sense of afʿal is answered by citing authorities like Abu ʿUbayda and Prophetic usage where mawlā means 'the one more entitled (awlā) and the owner of management', but it is argued that mawlā is a noun designating this meaning, not a comparative adjective like awlā.

Key Terms
المتواتر
الآحاد
صحة الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ghadir hadith is unanimously accepted as authentic by all Muslims.
Correction: The author shows that major Sunni hadith scholars did not transmit it and some criticized it, so it is not universally accepted.

Checkpoint
What is one of the main objections to the authenticity of the Ghadir hadith mentioned by the author?
A) It is not recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim.
B) It was fabricated by the Shia.
C) The chain includes unknown narrators.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The authenticity of the Ghadir hadith is contested; it is not found in Bukhari and Muslim and was criticized by hadith scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a narration you have always believed to be authentic is challenged by reliable scholars, would you investigate the evidence or cling to your initial belief? What does that say about your trust in truth over tradition?
?
The author calls the claim of tawātur 'مكابرة' (stubborn denial). Are there areas in your own faith where you refuse to look at evidence because it might unsettle your comfort? Will you commit to seeking truth even when it is uncomfortable?
?
When a hadith seems to support your sect or group, do you accept it more readily? How would you react if you discovered that your position is based on weak or disputed evidence? Are you willing to align your conviction solely with the strongest proof, even if it costs you community approval?

The Meaning of Mawlā and the Missing Preamble
Linguistic analysis of 'mawlā' in the Ghadir hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic arguments that mawlā does not mean awlā (more entitled) in the Ghadir hadith.

Core Idea
Mawlā means helper, not more entitled.

Even if the Ghadir hadith were authentic, the key word 'mawlā' can be interpreted in multiple ways. The author argues that the word means 'helper' (nāṣir) based on the rest of the hadith and linguistic evidence. He also points out that most narrators omit the crucial preamble that would connect mawlā to the meaning of awlā.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the argument about the missing preamble and the linguistic points about mawlā.
2
Understand the preamble objection
The author says most narrators do not include the sentence 'Am I not more entitled to you than yourselves?' This sentence is needed to interpret mawlā as awlā.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
The author cites the rest of the hadith (wa man wālāhu) to show mawlā means helper. Also note the grammatical pattern (mufʿal not afʿal) and the usage examples.
4
Consider the Quranic verse
The author uses Quran 29:25 to show mawlā can mean abode/destination, not priority. This supports the lexical range of the word.

الحمل عليها وجعل اللفظ حقيقة في هذا القدر المشترك (دفعاً للاشتراك) اللفظي . ( الجواب : منع صحة الحديث ودعوى الضرورة) في العلم بصحته لكونه متواتراً مكابرة كيف ولم ينقله أكثر أصحاب الحديث) كالبخاري ومسلم وأضرابهما، وقد طعن بعضهم فيه كابن أبي داود السجستاني وأبي حاتم الرازي وغيرهما من أئمة الحديث، (ولأن عليا لم يكن يوم الغدير مع النبي فإنه كان باليمن، ورد هذا بأن غيبته لا تنافي صحة الحديث إلا أن يروى هكذا: أخذ بيد علي أو استحضره وقال : ( وإن سلم ( أن هذا الحديث صحيح ( فرواته ) أي : أكثرهم (لم يرووا مقدمة الحديث وهي ألست أولى بكم من أنفسكم فلا يمكن أن يتمسك بها في أن المولى بمعنى الأولى . ( والمراد بالمولى ) هو ( الناصر بدليل آخر الحديث ) وهو قوله : ( وال من والاه إلخ ) ، ولأن مفعل بمعنى أفعل لم يذكره أحد ) من أئمة العربية وقوله تعالى : ومأواكم النار ) [العنكبوت : ٢٥ ]، هي مولاكم، أي: مقركم وما إليه مآلكم وعاقبتكم ولهذا قال الله تعالى : وبئس المصير [ الحديد : ١٥ ] ، وقد قيل : المراد هاهنا أيضا الناصر فيكون مبالغة في نفي النصرة على طريقة قولهم : الجوع زاد من لا زاد له ( و ) الاستعمال أيضا يدل على أن المولى ليس بمعنى الأولى . ( لجواز) أن يقال : ( هو أولى من كذا دون مولى من كذا و) أن يقال ( أولى الرجلين أو الرجال دون مولى ( الرجلين أو الرجال ( وإن سلم ) أن المولى بمعنى الأولى ( فأين الدليل على أن المراد الأولى بالتصرف والتدبير بل يجوز أن يراد الأولى ( في أمر من الأمور كما قال الله تعالى : إن أولى الناس بإبراهيم للذين اتبعوه ﴾ [آل عمران : ٦٨]، وأراد الأولوية في الاتباع والاختصاص به والقرب منه لا في التصرف فيه ) وتقول التلامذة : نحن أولى بأستاذنا ويقول الأتباع : نحن أولى بسلطاننا ) ولا يريدون الأولوية في التصرف والتدبير بل في أمر ما والصحة ( الاستفسار ) إذ يجوز أن يقال في أي شيء هو أولى في نصرته أو محبته أو التصرف فيه (و) لصحة ( التقسيم) بأن يقال : كون فلان أولى بزيد إما في نصرته وإما في ضبط أمواله وإما في تدبيره والتصرف فيه وحينئذ لا يدل الحديث على إمامته.
قوله : ( ولأن مفعل بمعنى أفعل لم يذكره أحد أجيب عنه بأن المولى بمعنى المتولي والمالك للأمر، والأولى بالتصرف شائع في كلام العرب منقول من أئمة اللغة، قال أبو عبيدة هي موليك أي أولى بكم، وقال عليه السلام أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن مولاها أي الأولى بها، والمالك لتدبير أمرها ثم المراد أنه اسم لهذا المعنى لا صفة بمنزلة الأولى ليعترض بأنه ليس من صيفة اسم التفضيل وأنه لا يستعمل استعماله .
The Fourth Observatory - The Fourth Objective: The Imam after the Messenger of Allah. [It is argued] to apply [the word] to this common meaning and to make the word literal in this common denominator (to avoid verbal homonymy). (The answer: denial of the authenticity of the hadith and the claim of necessity) of knowledge of its authenticity due to its being mutawātir is a stubborn [claim]—how [can it be] when most hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim and their peers did not transmit it? Indeed, some of them criticized it, such as Ibn Abī Dāwūd al-Sijistānī and Abū Ḥātim al-Rāzī and other imams of hadith. (And because ʿAlī was not with the Prophet on the day of Ghadīr, for he was in Yemen. This was refuted by [saying] that his absence does not negate the authenticity of the hadith unless it is narrated thus: 'He took ʿAlī's hand' or 'summoned him and said...'). (Even if it is granted) that this hadith is authentic, (its narrators)—i.e., most of them—(did not narrate the preamble of the hadith, namely 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' So it is not possible to rely on it for [the claim] that mawlā means awlā. (What is meant by al-mawlā) is (al-nāṣir [the helper], as evidenced by the rest of the hadith), namely his saying: 'And the mawlā of whoever takes him as mawlā,' etc. And because the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal has not been mentioned by anyone among the authorities on Arabic. And His exalted saying: 'and your abode is the Fire' [al-ʿAnkabūt 29:25] 'are your mawlā', meaning your abode and your ultimate destination and end, which is why God said: 'and the evil destination' [al-Ḥadīd 57:15]. And it has been said: what is meant here is also al-nāṣir, so it would be an exaggeration in negating support, in the manner of their saying: 'Hunger is the provision of one who has no provision.' (And) usage also indicates that mawlā is not in the sense of awlā. (Because it is permissible) to say: 'He is awlā (more entitled) than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so', and it is permissible to say 'the awlā of the two men' or 'of the men' but not 'the mawlā of the two men' or 'of the men'. (Even if it is granted) that mawlā means awlā, (where is the proof that what is meant is the awlā in disposal and administration? Rather, it is possible that what is meant is awlā in some matter, as God said: 'Indeed, the people most entitled (awlā) to Abraham are those who followed him' [Āl ʿImrān 3:68], and He meant priority in following, special attachment to him, and closeness to him, not disposal over him.) And the students say: 'We are awlā to our teacher,' and the followers say: 'We are awlā to our sultan,' and they do not mean priority in disposal and administration, but in some matter. And [what supports it is] the validity of (istifsār [asking for clarification]) because it is possible to say: 'In what thing is he awlā? In his support, his love, or disposal over him?' (And) the validity of (taqsīm [division]) because it is possible to say: 'So-and-so's being awlā to Zayd is either in his support, or in controlling his wealth, or in his management and disposal over him.' And then the hadith does not indicate his imāma. His saying: 'And because no one mentioned the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal'—it was answered that al-mawlā means al-mutawallī (the one who takes charge) and al-mālik (the owner) of the affair, and al-awlā bi-l-taṣarruf (more entitled to disposal) is common in Arab speech, transmitted from language scholars. Abu ʿUbayda said: 'hiya mawlīkum' meaning 'awla bikum' (more entitled to you), and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her mawlā' meaning the one more entitled to her (awlā bihā), the owner of the management of her affair. Then what is meant is that it is a noun for this meaning, not a descriptive term like awlā, so that it might be objected that it is not from the form of the elative (ism al-tafḍīl) and that it is not used as they are used.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the Shia argument that the hadith of Ghadīr (man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu) proves that ʿAlī is the rightful imam after the Prophet. The author presents a series of objections and answers. First, he denies the hadith's authenticity, pointing out that many major hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim did not transmit it, and some even criticized it. He also notes that ʿAlī was not present at Ghadīr Khumm because he was in Yemen, though this is countered by saying absence does not preclude the hadith being narrated otherwise. Second, even if the hadith is authentic, most narrators omit the preamble 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' which is crucial for interpreting mawlā as 'more entitled' (awla). Third, the word mawlā in the hadith means 'helper' (nāṣir), as indicated by the continuation of the hadith. Fourth, linguistically, the pattern mufʿal does not mean afʿal, and usage shows that mawlā is not synonymous with awlā: one can say 'He is awlā than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so'. Fifth, even if mawlā is taken to mean awlā, it could refer to priority in some specific matter like support or love, not necessarily in authority and disposal. The author illustrates this with examples from the Quran and common speech. Finally, a linguistic objection about mufʿal being in the sense of afʿal is answered by citing authorities like Abu ʿUbayda and Prophetic usage where mawlā means 'the one more entitled (awlā) and the owner of management', but it is argued that mawlā is a noun designating this meaning, not a comparative adjective like awlā.

Key Terms
المولى
الناصر
الاشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author give for the meaning of 'mawlā' as 'helper'?
A) The preamble of the hadith.
B) The rest of the hadith: 'wa man wālāhu'.
C) The Quranic verse in Surah Al-Imran.
D) The use of mawlā in poetry.

Key Takeaway
The word 'mawlā' in the Ghadir hadith most likely means 'helper', not 'more entitled', based on the hadith itself, linguistic patterns, and Quranic usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a familiar hadith, do you accept its popular interpretation without question, or do you examine the linguistic details as this lesson does? This lesson reveals that a single word can shift the entire meaning. What other teachings might you have accepted without verifying the original Arabic? Will you commit to studying primary sources more carefully?
?
The author shows that a missing preamble changes the meaning of 'mawlā'. Are there beliefs you hold that rely on incomplete proofs or missing context? This lesson calls you to be honest about the strength of your evidence. Will you re-evaluate your positions with the same rigor?
?
If 'mawlā' means helper rather than master, how does that affect your view of religious authority? Are you quick to appoint leaders based on ambiguous texts? This lesson demands precision. Will you hold yourself and your scholars to the same standard of linguistic accuracy?

The Ambiguity of Awlā and Its Implications for Imamate
The ambiguity of 'awlā' and its inability to prove imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if mawlā means awlā, the hadith is ambiguous and does not necessarily prove political authority.

Core Idea
Priority can be in many matters, not just authority.

Even if we accept that mawlā means awlā (more entitled), the hadith still does not prove that Ali is entitled to political authority. The author demonstrates that 'awlā' is ambiguous; it could refer to support, love, or closeness, not necessarily leadership. He uses examples from the Quran and common speech to show that priority can be in various matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the argument that even if mawlā means awlā, the specific meaning is not determined.
2
Identify the Quranic and common examples
The author cites Quran 3:68 about Abraham, and the statements of students about their teacher and followers about their sultan.
3
Understand istifsār and taqsīm
The author argues that one can ask 'in what way is he awlā?' (istifsār) and divide the possibilities (taqsīm), showing ambiguity.
4
Connect to the overall refutation
Realize that the hadith, even if authentic and interpreted as awlā, does not specifically mean authority over the community.

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the Shia argument that the hadith of Ghadīr (man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu) proves that ʿAlī is the rightful imam after the Prophet. The author presents a series of objections and answers. First, he denies the hadith's authenticity, pointing out that many major hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim did not transmit it, and some even criticized it. He also notes that ʿAlī was not present at Ghadīr Khumm because he was in Yemen, though this is countered by saying absence does not preclude the hadith being narrated otherwise. Second, even if the hadith is authentic, most narrators omit the preamble 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' which is crucial for interpreting mawlā as 'more entitled' (awla). Third, the word mawlā in the hadith means 'helper' (nāṣir), as indicated by the continuation of the hadith. Fourth, linguistically, the pattern mufʿal does not mean afʿal, and usage shows that mawlā is not synonymous with awlā: one can say 'He is awlā than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so'. Fifth, even if mawlā is taken to mean awlā, it could refer to priority in some specific matter like support or love, not necessarily in authority and disposal. The author illustrates this with examples from the Quran and common speech. Finally, a linguistic objection about mufʿal being in the sense of afʿal is answered by citing authorities like Abu ʿUbayda and Prophetic usage where mawlā means 'the one more entitled (awlā) and the owner of management', but it is argued that mawlā is a noun designating this meaning, not a comparative adjective like awlā.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If mawlā means awlā, it must mean priority in authority.
Correction: The author shows that awlā can refer to many kinds of priority, and the hadith does not specify which.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that 'awlā' can mean priority in something other than authority?
A) 'The people most entitled to Abraham are those who followed him.'
B) 'The rich are most entitled to charity.'
C) 'The prophet is most entitled to the believers.'
D) 'The imam is most entitled to lead prayer.'

Key Takeaway
Even if mawlā means awlā, the hadith is ambiguous and does not prove Ali's political imamate.

Refutation of the Hadith of Manzila for Imamate
The author critiques the argument from the hadith comparing Ali to Harun's position relative to Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia argument from the Hadith of Manzila for Ali's right to caliphate and the Sunni refutation based on the hadith's authenticity, limited context, and lack of generality.

Core Idea
The Hadith of Manzila does not prove Ali's succession—it only refers to a temporary deputyship.

This lesson examines the second Sunni evidence used by Shia to claim Ali's right to caliphate: the famous hadith 'You are to me as Harun to Moses.' We will explore both the Shia interpretation that it grants Ali all of Harun's positions except prophethood, including succession after death, and the Sunni response that challenges the hadith's authenticity and its scope, arguing that it was specific to the Tabuk expedition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the argument and counterargument.
The passage contains the Shia claim, then the Sunni refutation in multiple parts.
2
Understand the Shia reasoning
Identify how the Shia use the hadith to argue that Ali should be the successor, taking all of Harun's positions except prophethood.
They argue that succession after death is among those positions.
3
Examine the Sunni refutation
Note the three main points: denial of authenticity, that the hadith is ahad and does not yield certainty, and that it refers only to the Tabuk context.
Pay attention to the argument that 'be my successor' does not imply permanence.
4
Reflect on key terms
Consider the terms 'المنزلة', 'الاستخلاف', and 'الآحاد' and how they shape the debate.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-sentence summary of the main takeaway from the refutation.

موسی
The Fourth Observatory - The Fourth Objective: The Imam after the Messenger of God ﷺ.
(The second) of the sunna evidences (his saying ﷺ to ʿAlī when he went out on the expedition of Tabūk and appointed him as his deputy over Medina: 'You are to me in the position of Hārūn to Mūsā, except that there is no prophet after me.') For it indicates that all the positions established for Hārūn from Mūsā, except prophethood, are established for ʿAlī from the Prophet ﷺ, for if the wording were not taken to apply to all the positions, (then among the positions established for Hārūn from Mūsā would be his deserving to take his place after his death if he had lived).
[There is a garbled phrase:] Meaning of the message, the exception is valid, not the inference, Hārūn after him, and that is because he was the khalīfa of Mūsā during his lifetime, as evidenced by his saying: 'Be my successor among my people' – for successorship except assuming the place of the one succeeded in what pertains to his disposals, so it was necessary that he be his successor after his death on the assumption of his survival. Otherwise, his dismissal would necessitate belittling him and aversion from him, and that is not permissible for prophets. (But that assumption of Mūsā's position) was his by virtue of the station in prophethood, and it is negated here by the evidence of the exception. Al-Āmidī said: The second aspect of the two aspects of this hadith is that among the positions of Hārūn with respect to Mūsā is that he was his partner in [prophethood?], and among its necessary consequences is his being owed obedience after the death of Mūsā had he remained, so it was necessary to establish that for ʿAlī, except that partnership in the message is prevented, so it was necessary that he remain obligated to be obeyed by the community after the Prophet ﷺ, acting on the evidence to the fullest extent possible. (The answer: denial of the authenticity of the hadith) as al-Āmidī denied it, and according to hadith scholars it is authentic although it is of the āḥād type. (Or) we say, assuming its authenticity: there is no generality in it regarding the positions, (rather what is meant is his deputyship over his people in his saying: 'Be my successor among my people' for his deputyship over Medina, meaning that the intent of the hadith is that ʿAlī is his deputy over Medina in the expedition of Tabūk, just as Hārūn was the deputy of Mūsā over his people during his absence. (And its permanence does not follow), i.e., the permanence of Mūsā's deputyship after his death, for his saying 'be my successor' has no generality such that it necessitates deputyship in every time, but rather the apparent meaning is his deputyship during the period of his absence. (And thus) (the non-permanence after the death of Mūsā, due to the deficiency of the wording's indication of his deputyship therein, would not be) a dismissal of him, just as if he had explicitly restricted the deputyship to some actions and not others. (And there is no dismissal if there is a change?)
(
His statement: ('to take his place') i.e., by way of designation, for if Hārūn had lived after the death of Mūsā, his caliphate would have been designated. And to this extent, if it were established, their claim would be established, and what is said is not refuted: its utmost is the indication of his deserving the imāma, not the negation of the imāma of the three imams before him. So reflect.
His statement: ('al-Āmidī said: the second aspect, etc.') He only cited his statement because the author's statement in the answer this and the enforcement of Hārūn's command, etc., only fits this exposition, not the author's own exposition. His statement: 'and among its necessary consequences is being owed obedience, etc.') There is in it that among the necessary consequences of being owed obedience is also during his lifetime, and the consequent is false in the case of ʿAlī (may God be pleased with him), and it may be answered that it is not possible to act on the evidence regarding the time of the Prophet ﷺ because of decisive proofs, so it remains operative regarding the time of death.
Mūsā.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents and then refutes the Shia argument from the second sunna evidence: the hadith 'You are to me in the position of Hārūn to Mūsā, except that there is no prophet after me' (hadith al-manzila). The author first states the Shia claim that this hadith proves all of Hārūn's positions for ʿAlī except prophethood, including the right to succeed the Prophet after his death. The reasoning is that Hārūn was Mūsā's successor during his lifetime (deputy over his people) and would have been his successor after death had he lived, since dismissing him would be unbecoming. The Shia argue that ʿAlī therefore must be owed obedience after the Prophet's death. Then the author gives the rebuttal from al-Āmidī and the commentator: (1) the hadith's authenticity is denied by some scholars; (2) even if accepted, it is from āḥād reports and does not necessitate certainty; (3) the hadith has no general application to all positions—it specifically refers to ʿAlī's deputyship over Medina during the Tabūk expedition, not to perpetual succession; (4) the phrase 'be my successor' does not imply permanence after death, but only for the duration of the expedition. The author also addresses a subtle point: the claim that the hadith indicates ʿAlī's right to imāma does not negate the imāma of the previous three caliphs. Finally, a marginal note clarifies that the objection about obedience being owed during the Prophet's lifetime is invalid because the Prophet's lifetime excluded such obedience due to other definitive proofs.

Key Terms
المنزلة
الاستخلاف
الخلافة
الآحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Manzila proves that Ali was the designated successor after the Prophet.
Correction: The hadith, even if authentic, refers only to temporary deputyship over Medina during the Tabuk expedition, not to perpetual succession. The Sunni refutation shows that it does not establish imamate after the Prophet's death.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary interpretation of the hadith 'You are to me as Harun was to Moses'?
A) It establishes Ali as the Prophet's permanent successor.
B) It refers only to Ali's deputyship over Medina during the Tabuk expedition.
C) It proves Ali's superiority over all other companions.
D) It is a weak hadith with no significance.

Key Takeaway
The Hadith of Manzila is insufficient to prove Ali's post-Prophetic succession due to its limited context and lack of generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you compare the Shia and Sunni interpretations of the Hadith of Manzila, which one challenges your assumptions more? Are you willing to re-examine a hadith you once thought settled, even if it means rethinking your view of the Companions?
?
The Sunni response limits the hadith to a temporary deputyship. Does this limitation feel like a twisting of the text to you, or a careful reading? Are you ready to submit your emotions to the discipline of evidence, even if it weakens a narrative you find compelling?
?
If the Hadith of Manzila does not prove Ali's succession, then the legitimacy of the first three caliphs stands. Can you genuinely say you accept that without reservation? Search your heart: is there any part of you that clings to the Shia claim because it feels more spiritually comforting? Will you repent from that if it contradicts the truth?

First Proof for Abu Bakr's Caliphate: Quranic Promise
Introduces counter-arguments and presents first Quranic proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Quran 24:55 as a proof for the caliphate of Abu Bakr and the four rightly-guided caliphs.

Core Idea
Quran 24:55 promises succession to the righteous — and only the four caliphs fulfilled it.

After responding to the Shi'i arguments for Ali's imamate, the author now presents proofs for Abu Bakr's. The first proof is a Quranic verse promising succession to those who believe and do good. The author argues that this promise was fulfilled specifically in the caliphate of the four rightly-guided caliphs. This passage shows how scriptural interpretation is used to support historical claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Begin by reading Quran 24:55 carefully. Notice that it promises succession (istikhlaaf) to those who believe and do righteous deeds.
The verse is addressed to the Companions, as the author specifies.
2
Understand the author's interpretation
The author notes that the plural address implies a minimum of three people. Therefore the promise must be fulfilled in a group, not a single individual.
This interpretation supports the idea of a collective caliphate.
3
Identify the conclusion
The author concludes that only the caliphate of the four rightly-guided caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) matches this description, so they are the ones promised.
This directly counters claims that the verse refers to Ali alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد الأجوبة المفصلة على الوجوه المذكورة نقول: هذه النصوص التي تمسكوا بها في إمامة علي رضي الله عنه معارضة بالنصوص الدالة على إمامة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، وهي من وجوه الأول: قوله تعالى: {وعد الله الذين آمنوا منكم وعملوا الصالحات ليستخلفنهم في الأرض كما استخلف الذين من قبلهم وليمكنن لهم دينهم الذي ارتضى لهم} [النور: 55]، والخطاب للصحابة (وأقل الجمع ثلاثة [؟] الله حق) فوجب أن يوجد في جماعة خلافة يتمكن بها الدين (ولم يوجد على هذه الصفة) إلا خلافة الخلفاء الأربعة فهي التي وعد الله بها.
And after the detailed answers to the aforementioned aspects, we say: These texts that they clung to for the imamate of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, are opposed by texts indicating the imamate of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, and they are from aspects: The first: His saying, Exalted is He: {Allah has promised those who have believed among you and done righteous deeds that He will surely grant them succession [to authority] upon the earth just as He granted it to those before them and that He will surely establish for them their religion which He has approved for them} [An-Nur: 55], and the address is to the Companions (and the minimum of a plural is three, [؟] Allah's promise is true), so it is necessary that there exist in a group a succession by which the religion is established, and it has not existed in this description except the succession of the four caliphs, so they are the ones whom Allah promised.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage introduces the counter-arguments to the Shi'i proofs for Ali's imamate. After responding to those proofs, the author presents texts supporting Abu Bakr's imamate. The first proof is from Quran 24:55, which promises succession (istikhlaaf) to those who believe and do good. The author specifies that the address is to the Companions, and because the minimum plural is three, there must be a group caliphate. He asserts that only the caliphate of the four rightly-guided caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) fulfills this description, thus they are the ones Allah promised. The argument follows the author's order: first the verse, then its interpretation, then the conclusion that the four caliphs are the promised successors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse is sometimes misunderstood as referring to any caliph or specifically to Ali alone.
Correction: The author restricts the promise to the four rightly-guided caliphs collectively, based on the plural address and the historical fulfillment.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the minimum number required for the plural in the verse's address to the Companions?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The first proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate is based on Quran 24:55, interpreted to refer to the four rightly-guided caliphs collectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic promises, do you automatically assume they apply to you personally, or do you consider the historical context and specific conditions? How might this verse's interpretation challenge you to examine your own understanding of leadership in Islam?
?
The author argues that the promise was fulfilled in the four caliphs. Reflect on your own life: Are you actively seeking to be among those who are 'believers who do righteous deeds' so that you may be included in divine promises? Or do you take such promises for granted?
?
How does this lesson affect your view of early Islamic leadership disputes? Are you willing to accept that the evidence points to Abu Bakr's legitimacy, even if it challenges your preconceived notions?

Second Proof: The Caller is One of the Three Caliphs
Argues that the caller in Quran 48:16 is not the Prophet, Ali, or later rulers, but one of the three caliphs, specifically Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof from Quran 48:16 establishes that the caller to fight a mighty people must be one of the three caliphs, specifically Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The Quranic call to fight was issued by Abu Bakr, not the Prophet or Ali.

This lesson presents a key theological argument from the commentary on al-Mawaqif. The author uses a verse from Surat al-Fath to argue that the commander who called the bedouins to fight after the Prophet's death was one of the first three caliphs. By excluding the Prophet, Ali, and later rulers, the passage demonstrates that the call must have come from Abu Bakr, the first caliph.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and context
Begin by reading Quran 48:16, which addresses those who remained behind and promises a good reward if they obey the caller. Notice the contingent promise indicates the caller is a divinely appointed authority.
The verse is part of the aftermath of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah.
2
Understand why the Prophet is excluded
The author cites Quran 48:15 to show that the Prophet foretold the bedouins would never follow him again, so he could not be the caller. Also, in his lifetime, they were not called to fight.
This eliminates the most obvious candidate.
3
Examine why Ali is excluded
Ali's wars as caliph were for the imamate (e.g., fighting rebels), not for calling to Islam directly. The verse's context is fighting for faith, not political authority.
Ali's battles were against Muslims, not polytheists.
4
Discuss the exclusion of later rulers
Later rulers are considered oppressors (by Sunni view) or disbelievers (by Kharijite view), so the promise of divine reward for obedience would not apply.
This limits the caller to the early caliphs.
5
Conclude the caller and its implication
The caller is one of the three caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman). Since there is no basis for differentiation among them, the first in order, Abu Bakr, is the intended caller. The people are identified as Banu Hanifa (apostates).
This establishes the proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: قوله تعالى: {قل للمخلفين من الأعراب ستدعون إلى قوم أولي بأس شديد تقاتلونهم أو يسلمون} [الفتح: 16]، وليس الداعي إلى هؤلاء القوم لطلب الإسلام محمداً عليه السلام لقوله تعالى: {سيقول المخلفون} [الفتح: 11]، إلى قوله: {قل لن تتبعونا كذلكم قال الله من قبل} [الفتح: 15] فقد علم النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم من هذه الآية أنهم لا يتبعون أبداً فكيف يدعوهم إلى القتال وأيضاً فإن المخلفين لم يدعوا إلى المحاربة في حياته عليه السلام ولا علياً لأنه لم يتفق له في أيام خلافته قتال لطلب الإسلام بل لطلب الإمامة ورعاية حقوقها ولا من بعده من الولاة والحكام لأنهم عندنا ظلمة وعندهم كفار فلا يليق بهم قوله: {فإن تطيعوا يؤتكم الله أجراً حسناً} [الفتح: 16] الآية، فهو أي ذلك الداعي الذي يجب باتباعه الأجر الحسن وبتركه العذاب الشديد أحد الخلفاء الثلاثة ويلزم خلافة أبي بكر لعدم القائل بالفصل، بل الظاهر أنه أبو بكر وأن القوم المذكورين بنو حنيفة أصحاب مسيلمة. قوله: (وإن القوم المذكورين بنو حنيفة وكانوا قد ارتدوا قال الله تعالى: {يقاتلونهم ويسلمون} [الفتح: 11] فإن المرتد لا يقبل منه إلا الإسلام أو السيف وقيل: المراد بالقوم المذكورين فارس أو الروم ومعنى يسلمون ينقادون لأن الروم نصارى وفارس محوس فيقبل منهم الجزية وإن لم يقبل من مشركي العرب اتفاقاً ومن مشركي العجم أيضاً عند الشافعي رحمه الله.
The second: His saying, Exalted is He: {Say to those who remained behind of the bedouins, 'You will be called to [fight] a people of severe might; you will fight them or they will submit.'} [Al-Fath: 16], and the caller to these people for the purpose of seeking Islam is not Muhammad, peace be upon him, because of His saying, Exalted is He: {Those who remained behind will say} [Al-Fath: 11] up to His saying: {Say, 'You will not follow us; thus has Allah said before.'} [Al-Fath: 15]. For the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, knew from this verse that they would never follow, so how could he call them to fighting? Also, those who remained behind were not called to battle in his lifetime, peace be upon him, nor was Ali, because in his caliphate he did not fight for the purpose of seeking Islam, but rather for seeking the imamate and guarding its rights. Nor [was the caller] from the governors and rulers after him, because according to us they are oppressors and according to them they are disbelievers, so His saying: {And if you obey, He will give you a good reward} [Al-Fath: 16] etc. is not befitting to them. So it (i.e., that caller, obeying whom yields a good reward and abandoning whom yields severe punishment) is one of the three caliphs, and the caliphate of Abu Bakr follows necessarily due to the absence of an advocate for differentiation. Rather, it is evident that it is Abu Bakr and that the aforementioned people are Banu Hanifa, the companions of Musaylima. His saying: (And that the aforementioned people are Banu Hanifa and they had apostatized. Allah said: {They fight them or they submit} [Al-Fath: 11]. For the apostate accepts nothing but Islam or the sword. And it is said: the meaning of the aforementioned people is Persia or Rome, and the meaning of 'they submit' is they surrender, because the Romans are Christians and the Persians are Magians, so jizya is accepted from them, although it is not accepted from the polytheists of the Arabs by agreement, and also not from the polytheists of the non-Arabs according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him.)
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate from Quran 48:16. The author argues that the caller to the mighty people is not the Prophet Muhammad because other verses indicate those left behind would never follow him. It is also not Ali because his wars were for the imamate, not for seeking Islam, and not later rulers because they are considered oppressors. Therefore, the caller must be one of the first three caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman), and since there is no distinction among them, the implication is that Abu Bakr is the first. The passage concludes with a footnote identifying the people as Banu Hanifa (followers of Musaylima) or alternatively Persians/Romans, discussing rulings on jizya.

Key Terms
داعي
خلافة
أبو بكر
بنو حنيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the Prophet himself was the caller to fight the mighty people mentioned in Quran 48:16.
Correction: The author proves the Prophet cannot be the caller because the preceding verses indicate the bedouins would never follow him, and they were not called to fight in his lifetime.

Checkpoint
Why does the author exclude Ali from being the caller?
A) Ali was not a caliph
B) His wars were for the imamate, not for seeking Islam
C) The verse was revealed after Ali's death
D) Ali refused to fight the bedouins

Key Takeaway
By process of elimination, the caller promised in Quran 48:16 can only be Abu Bakr, establishing a key proof for his caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Who is the authority in your life that you are called to obey, and do you truly see that obedience as a path to divine reward? What would it cost you to disobey?
?
The bedouins refused to follow the Prophet and were later called to fight under Abu Bakr. Have you ever resisted a clear command from Allah or His Messenger because it was inconvenient? What would it take for you to submit fully?
?
If the promise of severe punishment for abandoning the caller is real, then where in your life are you leaving the call of Allah unanswered? What is the first step you will take today to respond?

Identifying the Mighty People: Banu Hanifa and Jizya Rulings
Discusses the identity of the 'mighty people' as Banu Hanifa (apostates) or Persians/Romans, with implications for jizya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main interpretations of the 'mighty people' in Quran 48:16 and how they affect the ruling on accepting jizya.

Core Idea
The identity of the people determines whether they are apostates requiring sword or polytheists eligible for jizya.

The previous lesson established that the caller is Abu Bakr. This lesson delves into who exactly the 'mighty people' were. The passage presents two views: the primary view that they were Banu Hanifa, apostates from Musaylima's tribe, and an alternative view that they were Persians or Romans. This distinction has important implications for the permissibility of jizya.

Guided Path
1
The first interpretation: Banu Hanifa as apostates
The passage states that the people were Banu Hanifa, who had apostatized after the Prophet's death. For apostates, the only options are to return to Islam or face the sword.
This aligns with the well-known battle against Musaylima.
2
The second interpretation: Persians or Romans
Alternatively, some said the people were from Persia or Rome. In this case, 'submission' means surrender, and jizya is accepted from them because they are People of the Book (Romans) or Magians (Persians).
This interpretation changes the ruling entirely.
3
Contrast jizya rulings for Arabs vs. non-Arabs
The passage notes that jizya is not accepted from Arab polytheists by agreement, and not from non-Arab polytheists according to al-Shafi'i. This shows a scholarly difference.
The footnote reflects legal nuance within Sunni schools.
4
Understand the significance of the footnote
This footnote is appended to the main proof to address a potential objection: if the people were non-Arab polytheists, calling them to Islam vs. jizya would still be valid, but the earlier identification as Banu Hanifa is preferred.
The author likely includes this to preempt alternative interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: قوله تعالى: {قل للمخلفين من الأعراب ستدعون إلى قوم أولي بأس شديد تقاتلونهم أو يسلمون} [الفتح: 16]، وليس الداعي إلى هؤلاء القوم لطلب الإسلام محمداً عليه السلام لقوله تعالى: {سيقول المخلفون} [الفتح: 11]، إلى قوله: {قل لن تتبعونا كذلكم قال الله من قبل} [الفتح: 15] فقد علم النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم من هذه الآية أنهم لا يتبعون أبداً فكيف يدعوهم إلى القتال وأيضاً فإن المخلفين لم يدعوا إلى المحاربة في حياته عليه السلام ولا علياً لأنه لم يتفق له في أيام خلافته قتال لطلب الإسلام بل لطلب الإمامة ورعاية حقوقها ولا من بعده من الولاة والحكام لأنهم عندنا ظلمة وعندهم كفار فلا يليق بهم قوله: {فإن تطيعوا يؤتكم الله أجراً حسناً} [الفتح: 16] الآية، فهو أي ذلك الداعي الذي يجب باتباعه الأجر الحسن وبتركه العذاب الشديد أحد الخلفاء الثلاثة ويلزم خلافة أبي بكر لعدم القائل بالفصل، بل الظاهر أنه أبو بكر وأن القوم المذكورين بنو حنيفة أصحاب مسيلمة. قوله: (وإن القوم المذكورين بنو حنيفة وكانوا قد ارتدوا قال الله تعالى: {يقاتلونهم ويسلمون} [الفتح: 11] فإن المرتد لا يقبل منه إلا الإسلام أو السيف وقيل: المراد بالقوم المذكورين فارس أو الروم ومعنى يسلمون ينقادون لأن الروم نصارى وفارس محوس فيقبل منهم الجزية وإن لم يقبل من مشركي العرب اتفاقاً ومن مشركي العجم أيضاً عند الشافعي رحمه الله.
The second: His saying, Exalted is He: {Say to those who remained behind of the bedouins, 'You will be called to [fight] a people of severe might; you will fight them or they will submit.'} [Al-Fath: 16], and the caller to these people for the purpose of seeking Islam is not Muhammad, peace be upon him, because of His saying, Exalted is He: {Those who remained behind will say} [Al-Fath: 11] up to His saying: {Say, 'You will not follow us; thus has Allah said before.'} [Al-Fath: 15]. For the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, knew from this verse that they would never follow, so how could he call them to fighting? Also, those who remained behind were not called to battle in his lifetime, peace be upon him, nor was Ali, because in his caliphate he did not fight for the purpose of seeking Islam, but rather for seeking the imamate and guarding its rights. Nor [was the caller] from the governors and rulers after him, because according to us they are oppressors and according to them they are disbelievers, so His saying: {And if you obey, He will give you a good reward} [Al-Fath: 16] etc. is not befitting to them. So it (i.e., that caller, obeying whom yields a good reward and abandoning whom yields severe punishment) is one of the three caliphs, and the caliphate of Abu Bakr follows necessarily due to the absence of an advocate for differentiation. Rather, it is evident that it is Abu Bakr and that the aforementioned people are Banu Hanifa, the companions of Musaylima. His saying: (And that the aforementioned people are Banu Hanifa and they had apostatized. Allah said: {They fight them or they submit} [Al-Fath: 11]. For the apostate accepts nothing but Islam or the sword. And it is said: the meaning of the aforementioned people is Persia or Rome, and the meaning of 'they submit' is they surrender, because the Romans are Christians and the Persians are Magians, so jizya is accepted from them, although it is not accepted from the polytheists of the Arabs by agreement, and also not from the polytheists of the non-Arabs according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him.)
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate from Quran 48:16. The author argues that the caller to the mighty people is not the Prophet Muhammad because other verses indicate those left behind would never follow him. It is also not Ali because his wars were for the imamate, not for seeking Islam, and not later rulers because they are considered oppressors. Therefore, the caller must be one of the first three caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman), and since there is no distinction among them, the implication is that Abu Bakr is the first. The passage concludes with a footnote identifying the people as Banu Hanifa (followers of Musaylima) or alternatively Persians/Romans, discussing rulings on jizya.

Key Terms
بنو حنيفة
جزية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think jizya is universally accepted from all polytheists.
Correction: The passage clarifies that jizya is not accepted from Arab polytheists by consensus, and al-Shafi'i extends this to non-Arab polytheists.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for Banu Hanifa if they are the 'mighty people'?
A) They must pay jizya
B) They must accept Islam or be fought
C) They are to be left alone
D) They are to be exiled

Key Takeaway
The identity of the 'mighty people' in Quran 48:16 is either Banu Hanifa (apostates) or Persians/Romans, leading to different rulings on jizya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were among the Banu Hanifa who apostatized, what would you hope for? The sword or mercy? This lesson says only Islam or the sword. How does that make you feel about your own commitment to Islam? Are there areas where you have 'apostatized' in practice? Will you renew your intention today?
?
The lesson shows two scholarly interpretations of the same verse. Are you comfortable with such ambiguity? When you encounter differing Islamic views, do you seek to understand both sides or cling to one? How can you cultivate trust in scholars while recognizing legitimate differences?
?
The alternative interpretation allows jizya from Persians and Romans but not from Arab polytheists. Have you ever treated people differently based on their background or labels? How does this lesson challenge you to see the nuances in Islamic rulings and apply justice without bias?

The Third Proof: Abu Bakr's High Status Validates His Imamate
Deductive argument linking Abu Bakr's exaltation to his imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author uses Abu Bakr's high status with Allah as a proof for the validity of his caliphate and how the argument is structured logically.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's exaltation by Allah proves the legitimacy of his imamate.

This lesson presents a concise argument from the Sunnī position that Abū Bakr's high status with Allah directly implies the validity of his caliphate. The reasoning is simple yet powerful: if his leadership were invalid, he would not have been so magnified. But the umma universally recognizes his unparalleled virtue. Let's examine the logical flow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once. Notice the conditional statement and the factual claim.
Pay attention to the logical connector 'law' (if) and 'lākinnahu' (but he).
2
Identify the logical structure
Recognize that the argument is a modus tollens: If A then B, but not B, therefore not A. Here A = 'imamate invalid', B = 'not magnified'.
The conclusion is implicit: the author states the two premises.
3
Examine the premises
First premise: If imamate invalid, then no magnification. Second premise: But Abu Bakr is magnified and best of creation after the Prophet. Confirm that both are accepted by Sunnīs.
The second premise is considered unquestionable by the author.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on why the author links divine favor to political authority. What does this reveal about the relationship between spiritual status and leadership?
5
Note the promise of further explanation
The author promises more detail in the discussion of superiority (afḍaliyya). This shows the argument is part of a larger framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: لو كانت إمامة أبي بكر باطلة لما كان أبو بكر معظماً ممدوحاً عند الله لكنه معظم وأفضل الخلق عنده بعد رسول الله وسنزيده شرحاً وبياناً في مسألة الأفضلية.
The third: If the imamate of Abu Bakr were invalid, Abu Bakr would not have been magnified and praised in the sight of Allah. But he is magnified and the best of creation in His sight after the Messenger of Allah, and we will give it further explanation and clarification in the issue of superiority.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents a simple deductive argument: Abu Bakr is highly praised and considered the best of creation after the Prophet in Islamic tradition. If his imamate were invalid, such praise would be inconsistent. Therefore, his imamate must be valid. The author promises further elaboration on the issue of superiority (afdaliyya). The argument follows the author's order: the condition (if invalid), the consequent (no magnification), the fact (he is magnified), and the conclusion (imamate valid).

Key Terms
إمامة
معظَم
أفضل الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Bakr's high status is unrelated to the validity of his political leadership.
Correction: The author argues that such high status from Allah would not exist if his imamate were invalid, implying a necessary connection between divine favor and rightful authority.

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure of the third proof?
A) If A then B, not B, therefore not A
B) If A then B, B, therefore A
C) If not A then not B, B, therefore A
D) A or B, not A, therefore B

Key Takeaway
This proof shows that the divine favor bestowed upon Abu Bakr requires the validity of his caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's honor is a sign of rightful leadership, how does that shape your attitude toward those He has chosen to lead you—past and present? Will you actively seek to recognize and support them, or do you allow your own preferences to cloud your judgment?
?
The proof states that Abu Bakr is magnified and the best after the Prophet. Does your heart magnify him as Allah does? What practical steps can you take today to internalize his exalted status and let it influence your loyalty and actions?
?
If your own status in Allah's sight were measured by the same standard—that your actions must align with divine favor—would you pass the test? In what area of your life are you most at risk of claiming allegiance to the truth while living as if it doesn’t matter?

Proof from the Companions' Address
Presents proof from Companions' address and Quranic verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate based on the Companions addressing him as 'Caliph of the Messenger of Allah' and the Quranic attestation to their truthfulness.

Core Idea
Truthful witness confirms Abu Bakr's rightful caliphate.

This lesson examines a concise but powerful proof for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The argument draws on the actual practice of the Companions, including Ali ibn Abi Talib, who addressed Abu Bakr as 'Khalifat Rasul Allah.' Since Allah Himself testified to the truthfulness of the Companions in the Quran, their address serves as reliable testimony to the validity of his caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the key terms: 'Khalifat Rasul Allah' and 'al-sadiqun' (the truthful).
The passage is a single sentence but carries a complete argument.
2
Understand the proof structure
The proof has three elements: (1) the Companions' action, (2) the Quranic verse attesting to their truthfulness, and (3) the conclusion that the caliphate is legitimate. Identify each part.
3
Connect the Quranic verse
Look up Quran 59:8 (Al-Hashr) to see the full context. The verse praises the Muhajirun (Emigrants) as truthful. The author applies this to all Companions involved in the event.
The verse is not explicitly about the caliphate but about the Emigrants' sincerity.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this proof is persuasive: truthful people cannot lie about a matter as important as leadership. The inclusion of Ali, a key figure, removes any doubt about partisan bias.
Remember the misconception: Some might claim the Companions could be mistaken, but the verse removes that possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: كانت الصحابة وعلي يقولون له: يا خليفة رسول الله وقد قال تعالى فيهم {أولئك هم الصادقون} [الحشر: 8]، فتكون خلافته حقاً.
The fourth: The Companions and Ali used to say to him: 'O Caliph of the Messenger of Allah,' and Allah, Exalted is He, said about them: {Those are the truthful} [Al-Hashr: 8], so his caliphate becomes true.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the practice of the Companions, including Ali himself. They addressed Abu Bakr as 'Caliph of the Messenger of Allah,' and Allah described them as truthful in the Quran. Since truthful people would not say something false, their address confirms the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The argument is straightforward: the Companions' testimony is reliable, and they unanimously affirmed his caliphate.

Key Terms
خليفة
الصادقون
صحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Companions could have been mistaken or biased when they addressed Abu Bakr as caliph.
Correction: The Quranic verse (59:8) testifies to the truthfulness of the Companions, so their address cannot be a mistake or lie. Their truthful nature guarantees the accuracy of their testimony.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does the fourth proof cite to support the trustworthiness of the Companions?
A) Al-Nur: 55
B) Al-Fath: 16
C) Al-Hashr: 8
D) Al-Fatiha: 1

Key Takeaway
The Companions' unanimous address of Abu Bakr as caliph, combined with the Quranic affirmation of their truthfulness, establishes the legitimacy of his caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you, like the Companions, a person whose testimony about others can be trusted because you are truthful? Reflect on a time you spoke about someone's authority or character—did your words align with the truth, even when it was difficult? What step will you take this week to become more truthful in your speech?
?
The Companions called Abu Bakr 'Caliph of the Messenger of Allah' without hesitation. If you were among them, would you have had the courage to acknowledge the rightful leader, even if your personal preference differed? Ask yourself: What prevents you from acknowledging the truth when it is clear?
?
Allah attests to the truthfulness of the Companions. Knowing that your own words are witnessed by Allah, how does this change the way you speak about others? Write down one change you will make in your daily conversations to ensure you are counted among the truthful.

Fifth Proof: The Ummah's Virtue Refutes Ali's Imamate
Presents a conditional argument from Quranic praise of the Ummah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the Quranic praise of the Muslim community as the best of nations precludes the possibility that they collectively failed to support the rightful Imam.

Core Idea
The Ummah cannot be evil; so they must have supported the rightful Imam.

This lesson examines a fifth proof from Sunni theologians. They argue that since the Quran calls the Muslim community 'the best of nations' who enjoin good and forbid evil, it is impossible that they would have collectively failed to support Ali if he were the rightful Imam. The argument takes the form of a conditional syllogism, defending the legitimacy of the first three caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If A, then B; but not B, so not A.'
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the premises and conclusion
The first part is the conditional: if Ali's imamate were true and the Ummah did not assist him, they would be the worst of nations. The second part denies that consequence: the Ummah is the best of nations. Therefore, the original assumption is false.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
3
Understand the Quranic basis
The claim 'they are the best nation' is taken directly from Quran 3:110. The author uses this as an undeniable premise to show that the Ummah's collective action cannot be sinful.
4
Reflect on the implication
If this argument holds, then the Ummah's acceptance of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman is proof that Ali was not the intended immediate successor.
This is a key point in Sunni-Sunni polemics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: لو كانت الإمامة حق علي ولم تعنه الأمة عليه كما تزعمون لكانوا شر الأمم لكنهم خير أمة يأمرون بالمعروف وينهون عن المنكر، كما دل عليه نص القرآن.
The fifth: If the imamate were the right of Ali and the nation did not assist him against it as you claim, they would be the worst of nations. But they are the best nation, enjoining good and forbidding evil, as indicated by the text of the Quran.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents an argument based on the Quranic description of the Muslim community as the best of nations. If Ali were the rightful Imam and the community failed to support him, they would be guilty of a grave sin, contradicting their status. Therefore, Ali's claim cannot be true, and the community's acceptance of Abu Bakr must be correct. The argument follows: if A (Ali's right) then B (Ummah evil), but not B, so not A.

Key Terms
خير أمة
أمر بالمعروف
نهي عن المنكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ummah could collectively err or commit a sin by failing to support the true Imam.
Correction: The author argues that the Ummah's divinely praised status in the Quran (best of nations, urging good and forbidding evil) makes such a collective failure impossible. Therefore, their historical consensus is a sign of divine guidance.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude would happen if Ali's imamate were right and the Ummah failed to assist him?
A) They would be forgiven by Allah
B) They would be the worst of nations
C) They would still be the best of nations
D) They would be like previous nations

Key Takeaway
The Quranic description of the Ummah as the best of nations is used to argue that they could not have collectively rejected the rightful Imam, thus supporting the Sunni position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Ummah as a whole is described as the best nation, do you genuinely believe that the majority of Muslims today are still on truth? Or do you sometimes doubt the collective guidance? How does that affect your trust in the community of believers?
?
The argument rests on the idea that the Ummah could not have collectively rejected the rightful Imam. What does that imply about your own responsibility to follow the consensus of the early community? Are you willing to accept their judgment even when it challenges your personal preferences?
?
You are part of this 'best nation' described in the Quran. How actively do you enjoin good and forbid evil in your daily life? Would your contribution to the Ummah's virtue be enough to maintain its status as the best? What steps will you take today to fulfill that description?
